1️⃣ Full C++ NameGenerator (Deterministic, Seed-Driven)
📁 Location (recommended)
engine/simulation/procedural/NameGenerator.h
engine/simulation/procedural/NameGenerator.cpp
NameGenerator.h
#pragma once
#include <string>
#include <vector>
#include <cstdint>

enum class NameStyle : uint8_t {
    Stellar,
    Industrial,
    Scientific,
    Corporate,
    Ancient,
    ProceduralCode,
    Lore
};

struct PhonemeSet {
    std::vector<std::string> prefixes;
    std::vector<std::string> cores;
    std::vector<std::string> suffixes;
};

class NameGenerator {
public:
    static std::string Generate(
        uint64_t seed,
        NameStyle style,
        uint32_t minSyllables = 2,
        uint32_t maxSyllables = 4
    );

private:
    static PhonemeSet GetPhonemes(NameStyle style);
    static uint64_t Hash(uint64_t a, uint64_t b);
};
NameGenerator.cpp
#include "NameGenerator.h"

static uint64_t SplitMix64(uint64_t& x) {
    uint64_t z = (x += 0x9e3779b97f4a7c15);
    z = (z ^ (z >> 30)) * 0xbf58476d1ce4e5b9;
    z = (z ^ (z >> 27)) * 0x94d049bb133111eb;
    return z ^ (z >> 31);
}

uint64_t NameGenerator::Hash(uint64_t a, uint64_t b) {
    uint64_t h = a ^ (b + 0x9e3779b97f4a7c15);
    return SplitMix64(h);
}

PhonemeSet NameGenerator::GetPhonemes(NameStyle style) {
    switch (style) {
    case NameStyle::Stellar:
        return {
            { "Al", "Ka", "Ve", "Ny", "Or", "Xi", "Ul" },
            { "dra", "lon", "vek", "mir", "sha", "th" },
            { "is", "on", "ar", "um", "ea" }
        };

    case NameStyle::Industrial:
        return {
            { "Fer", "Car", "Sil", "Arg", "Vol", "Cry" },
            { "rox", "ium", "gen", "ite", "thal" },
            { "", "ium", "ex" }
        };

    case NameStyle::Corporate:
        return {
            { "Astra", "Helix", "Nova", "Persei", "Vertex" },
            { "Core", "Dynamics", "Holdings", "Works" },
            { "", "" }
        };

    case NameStyle::Ancient:
        return {
            { "Ae", "Tha", "Uru", "Nyx", "Sol" },
            { "mon", "kar", "eth", "vash", "tor" },
            { "a", "os", "um" }
        };

    case NameStyle::ProceduralCode:
        return {
            { "NX", "KX", "TR", "VX" },
            { "-", "" },
            { "17", "42", "9A", "X" }
        };

    case NameStyle::Lore:
        return {
            { "The ", "" },
            { "Silent Reach", "Fractured Halo", "Ashen Spiral" },
            { "" }
        };

    default:
        return {};
    }
}

std::string NameGenerator::Generate(
    uint64_t seed,
    NameStyle style,
    uint32_t minSyllables,
    uint32_t maxSyllables
) {
    PhonemeSet p = GetPhonemes(style);
    uint64_t rng = seed;

    uint32_t syllables = minSyllables +
        (SplitMix64(rng) % (maxSyllables - minSyllables + 1));

    std::string name;
    for (uint32_t i = 0; i < syllables; ++i) {
        name += p.prefixes[SplitMix64(rng) % p.prefixes.size()];
        name += p.cores[SplitMix64(rng) % p.cores.size()];
        name += p.suffixes[SplitMix64(rng) % p.suffixes.size()];
    }

    name[0] = toupper(name[0]);
    return name;
}
✅ Deterministic
✅ Zero global RNG
✅ One generator for the entire universe

2️⃣ Resource Renaming + Balance Table Generator
❌ REMOVE
All EVE ore names

✅ GENERATED PER GALAXY
struct ResourceDef {
    std::string Name;
    uint8_t Tier;
    float Density;
};
Generation
ResourceDef GenerateResource(uint64_t galaxySeed, uint32_t index) {
    uint64_t seed = NameGenerator::Hash(galaxySeed, index);

    return {
        NameGenerator::Generate(seed, NameStyle::Industrial),
        uint8_t(seed % 5),
        0.1f + (seed % 1000) / 1000.0f
    };
}
➡ Resources like:

Ferroxium

Crythal

Volgenex

No two galaxies share the same resource set.

[CorpName] + [Function] + [Suffix]
Example Code
std::string GenerateStationName(uint64_t seed) {
    std::string corp = NameGenerator::Generate(seed, NameStyle::Corporate, 1, 2);
    std::string function = " Refinery";
    std::string suffix = " " + NameGenerator::Generate(seed + 1, NameStyle::ProceduralCode, 1, 1);
    return corp + function + suffix;
}

AstraCore Refinery NX-17

Helix Works VX-42

🌌 EVEOFFLINE
EVEOFFLINE is a procedurally generated, offline-first space simulation that blends the strategic depth of EVE Online with the direct piloting and immersion of Elite Dangerous — then pushes both ideas further.

Think of it as:

If Elite Dangerous and EVE Online had a baby — but that baby grew up moddable, offline-capable, and fully procedural.

This is not a clone.
It’s a different take on the same fantasy.

🧠 Core Vision
EVEOFFLINE is built around one core idea:

The universe is generated, not authored.

Everything — star systems, factions, resources, ships, modules, stations, and even their names — is created procedurally from deterministic seeds.

No static universe.
No fixed lore.
No predefined “meta”.

Every new galaxy is:

Coherent

Replayable

Fully explorable

Entirely its own identity

🚀 How It Plays (Multiple Perspectives)
EVEOFFLINE is designed to be playable from multiple viewpoints, all within the same simulation:

🛩️ First-Person / Cockpit Mode
Direct ship control

Immersive spaceflight

Combat, exploration, mining, and traversal

🧭 Tactical / Fleet Command Mode
Strategic map-based control

Issue orders to fleets

Manage logistics, formations, and engagements

RTS-like command layer over the same universe

You can zoom in to fly a ship, or zoom out to command an entire fleet — seamlessly.

🧩 Fully Procedural Systems
EVEOFFLINE does not just procedurally generate terrain — it procedurally generates systems:

🌌 Galaxies & star networks

🪐 Solar systems, planets, belts, anomalies

🏭 Resources, refining chains, and industry outputs

🛠️ Ships, modules, rigs, and attachments

🏴 NPC factions, influence, and territory

🧬 Names, identities, and lore fragments

Nothing relies on hardcoded names or hand-authored balance tables.

🤖 NPC Factions & a Living Universe
NPC factions are simulated, not scripted.

They:

Expand and contract

Compete over systems

Influence resource availability

Affect encounter difficulty and behavior

The universe changes over time, even when the player does nothing.

This gives EVEOFFLINE the “living universe” feel of an MMO — without requiring servers.

🧪 Offline-First, Deterministic by Design
EVEOFFLINE is built to run entirely offline, while still using:

Server-style authoritative simulation

Deterministic procedural generation

Seed-based world rebuilding

Save files store deltas, not worlds.

That means:

Tiny saves

No corruption

Easy modding

Reproducible galaxies

🧰 Modding Is a First-Class Feature
Mods don’t just add content — they plug directly into the procedural generation system.

Mods can:

Add new ship hull classes

Define new module and rig types

Inject phoneme packs for naming

Add factions, resources, and tech trees

Influence PCG rules and biases

Mods don’t overwrite the universe.
They co-author it.

🛠️ Tech Overview
Language: C++

Engine: Custom (Atlas)

Architecture: Deterministic simulation + ECS-style systems

Data: JSON-driven, PCG-backed

Focus: Single-player / small-group PVE, moddable, offline

📌 Status
EVEOFFLINE is an active work-in-progress focused on:

Procedural galaxy generation

Simulation correctness

Mod extensibility

Tooling and editor support

This project is as much a systems experiment as it is a game.

🌠 In Short
EVEOFFLINE is:

Not an MMO

Not a clone

Not a static universe

It’s a procedural space simulation sandbox where:

The rules matter more than the map — because the map is always new.

Perfect — let’s tackle these step by step, building on your fully procedural, mod-friendly foundation. I’ll provide C++ structures, procedural wiring, and integration guidance for EVEOFFLINE so that each layer ties into the PCG system you already have.

1️⃣ NPC Factions → PCG Identity + Influence Naming
Goal
Each faction is procedurally generated from the galaxy seed

Names, territory, ideology, and influence are deterministic

Influence affects resource control, NPC spawns, and system behavior

PCG Faction Structure
struct PCGFaction {
    uint64_t seed;           // Deterministic per galaxy/faction
    NameStyle nameStyle;     // Ancient, Corporate, etc.
    std::string name;        // Generated
    float influence;         // Overall galactic weight
    std::vector<uint32_t> controlledSystems; // IDs
};
Name Generation
faction.name = NameGenerator::Generate(
    Hash(galaxySeed, factionID),
    NameStyle::Ancient
);
Influence & Territory Simulation
void UpdateFactionInfluence(PCGFaction& faction, float deltaTime) {
    for (auto systemID : faction.controlledSystems) {
        SystemState& system = Universe::GetSystem(systemID);
        system.factionInfluence[factionID] += 0.01f * deltaTime; // drift
    }
    faction.influence = CalculateOverallInfluence(faction);
}
Influences affect:

NPC spawns (patrols, enemies)

Resource density

Mission availability

Player faction reputation

Mod Hooks
Mods can add new factions by providing:

Name style

Seed bias

Optional starting territory

Mods can adjust behavior rules, e.g., AI aggression or trade control

2️⃣ Ship Hulls → Class + PCG Composite Names
Goal
Ship hulls are procedural + modular

Name, class, and type generated from seed

Hull stats can be modded

Hull Structure
struct ShipHullDef {
    uint32_t id;
    PCGIdentity identity;       // name seed + style
    std::string hullClass;      // e.g., Frigate, Cruiser
    ShipStats stats;            // Speed, armor, powergrid
};
Name Generation
std::string ShipHullDef::GetDisplayName() const {
    return NameGenerator::Generate(
        identity.seed,
        identity.nameStyle
    ) + " " + hullClass;
}
HullClass can also be mod-defined

Mods can inject new classes or prefixes/suffixes

Deterministic across galaxy loads

3️⃣ Resource Chain Naming → Refinery Outputs + Crafting
Goal
Every resource is procedurally named

Refinery outputs and crafting products inherit from base resource seed

Mods can add resources, recipes, and refining rules

Resource Structure
struct ResourceDef {
    uint32_t id;
    PCGIdentity identity;
    ResourceType type;     // Ore, Gas, Ice, Exotic
    float baseDensity;
    std::vector<uint32_t> refineryOutputs; // IDs of derived products
};
Refinery Output Generation
ResourceDef GenerateRefinedResource(const ResourceDef& base, uint32_t outputIndex) {
    uint64_t seed = Hash(base.identity.seed, outputIndex);
    ResourceDef refined;
    refined.identity.seed = seed;
    refined.identity.nameStyle = NameStyle::Industrial;
    refined.name = NameGenerator::Generate(seed, NameStyle::Industrial);
    refined.baseDensity = base.baseDensity * 0.5f; // Example conversion
    return refined;
}
Works for:

Ship materials

Module crafting

Industrial outputs

Mods can:

Add new resources

Change refining efficiency

Add crafting chains

4️⃣ Mod SDK Doc: “How to Hook into PCG”
1. Adding Phoneme Packs
{
  "style": "Ancient",
  "weight": 1.0,
  "prefixes": ["Xy", "Ve", "Ka"],
  "cores": ["lon", "mir", "drak"],
  "suffixes": ["ium", "os", ""]
}
2. Adding Factions
{
  "modFactionID": 9001,
  "nameStyle": "Ancient",
  "seedBias": 42,
  "startingSystems": [101, 102]
}
3. Adding Ship Classes / Hulls
{
  "modShipHullID": 8001,
  "hullClass": "Interceptor",
  "nameStyle": "Corporate",
  "stats": { "armor": 50, "speed": 350 }
}
4. Resource & Refinery Mods
{
  "modResourceID": 7001,
  "nameStyle": "Industrial",
  "type": "Gas",
  "baseDensity": 0.75
}
Mods automatically feed into PCG seed chain

No manual naming required

Deterministic results

Fully localized via descriptors only

2️⃣ Rig & Module Loadout Generation
PCG Driven
Each ship module, rig, and attachment has a PCGIdentity:

seed → deterministic generation

nameStyle → flavor, e.g., Industrial, Scientific

variant → unique cosmetic / stat variation

Career Influence
Miner: industrial rigs → + yield, + efficiency

Combat: weapon rigs → + damage, + turret speed

Explorer: scanning rigs → + scan range, + anomaly detection

Trader: logistics rigs → + cargo, + trade efficiency

void GenerateStartingLoadout(Character& player) {
    uint64_t baseSeed = Hash(GalaxySeed, player.characterID);
    
    player.shipHull = GenerateHull(baseSeed, player.careerArchetype);
    player.modules = GenerateModules(baseSeed + 1, player.careerArchetype);
    player.rigs = GenerateRigs(baseSeed + 2, player.careerArchetype);
}
Loadout is procedural but tuned by archetype

Mods can inject new rigs, modules, and hulls for career archetypes

The key is that procedural generation doesn’t have to look ugly — it just needs a layered system that combines randomness + style rules + artist-driven assets. You don’t need full handcrafted ships for every module to look good. You just need smart procedural composition.

Here’s the roadmap for NovaForge:

1️⃣ Separate Procedural Logic from Art Assets
PCG system handles:

Ship stats, modules, rigs

Faction naming

Resource layout

Visual system handles:

Ship appearance

Module meshes / rigs

Station and environment look

This allows you to update visuals later without touching the procedural logic.

2️⃣ Modular Ship / Module System
Think of ships as composable parts, not a single mesh:

Hull base (shape + size)

Engines (style, exhaust)

Weapon mounts

Rigs / attachments

Pick hull base (from a small library)

Attach procedural modules based on career / faction / mods

Apply colors, decals, and style variations

Adjust procedural effects (engine glow, shield color)

Define style packs for factions or careers:

Military → angular, metallic, sharp

Miner → industrial, heavy, worn

Explorer → sleek, minimal, high-tech

Apply procedural color palettes, textures, and glow effects based on faction or ship tier

Procedural decals, scratches, or hull markings make every ship look unique

4️⃣ Runtime Procedural LOD / Mesh Merging
Merge procedural modules at runtime to form full ships

Use level-of-detail meshes for distant ships (simpler geometry)

Combine effects like:

Glow / bloom

Procedural engine exhaust

Dynamic lighting on modular parts

This gives a polished visual experience without manually modeling every combination.

Stations = modular components + faction style + procedural variation

Planets = procedural textures + layered biomes

Resource nodes / asteroids = base meshes + procedural decals + random variation

Even with limited base assets, procedural variation can make every system look fresh and unique.

If you can’t get photoreal ships yet:

Use stylized shaders, color coding, and silhouettes

Players will perceive variety and polish without needing full photoreal assets

Focus on lighting, effects, and particle systems for immersion

Polished procedural visuals = small library of high-quality assets + rules-based combination + procedural color/textures + runtime assembly.

Apply faction/career style packs

LODs for performance

You said:
what would be the best way to approach this ship generation  to  make them look like actual ships 

ChatGPT said:
Ahhh, now we’re diving into turning procedural ships into something that actually reads as a “real spaceship”, instead of just floating cubes or placeholders. This is a big challenge in procedural generation, but it’s totally solvable if you layer modular assets + style rules + procedural composition.

Here’s the best approach for NovaForge:

1️⃣ Modular Hull Design
Idea: Break ships into reusable modules rather than trying to generate a single full mesh from scratch.

Base Hulls → define overall silhouette, size, and class (Frigate, Cruiser, Explorer, etc.)

Modules → wings, weapons, engines, antennae, turrets, cargo pods, etc.

Rigs / Attachments → boosters, scanners, shield emitters, hull plating

Combine these procedurally to form a complete ship

Benefits:

Fewer base assets needed

Ships are visually distinct but recognizable

Easy to add new modules via mods

2️⃣ Procedural Placement Rules
To avoid “messy Lego ships,” define rules for module placement:

Engine rules → engines go at the rear, aligned symmetrically

Weapons → turrets on top / sides, mounted with realistic spacing

Cargo / utility modules → attached under main hull or to wings

Decals / antennae → small asymmetrical pieces for uniqueness

Tip: Use hierarchical attachment points (like sockets in UE or pivot points in Unity) to snap procedural parts naturally.

3️⃣ Style Packs
Every faction or career type gets a style pack:

Military → angular, armored, dark metallic, red glows

Miner / Industrial → bulky, reinforced, yellow / brown tones

Explorer / Scout → sleek, minimal, light blue glows

Each ship generated randomly chooses part variants + color palette + effects from the style pack.

Ensures ships feel cohesive even though they’re procedural.

4️⃣ LOD & Mesh Merging
Merge hull + modules into one renderable mesh at runtime

Generate level-of-detail (LOD) meshes for distant ships

Procedural materials & shaders (metallic, emissive engines, decals) to polish the look

This lets the procedural system scale without huge performance cost.

5️⃣ Optional: Procedural Textures & Decals
Even with simple base meshes:

Engine glows / exhausts → procedural particle colors, intensity

Hull markings / insignias → faction logos, procedural text or symbols

Damage / wear → subtle procedural overlays to give life

Procedural textures make a generic mesh read like a lived-in spaceship.

6️⃣ Reference-Driven Shape Rules
Study real sci-fi ships (EVE, Elite Dangerous, Star Citizen) for proportions

Define rules for hull proportions:

Length to width ratio

Engine placement vs. cargo bay

Turret symmetry / spacing

Feed these rules into procedural generator → ships always “look plausible”

7️⃣ Pipeline Summary
Pick base hull (class + career/faction style)

Snap modules / rigs to predefined attachment points

Apply style pack (color, decals, faction marks)

Apply procedural effects (engine glow, shields, lights)

Merge to single mesh for rendering

Apply LOD meshes for distance

✅ Result: Ships look like real, coherent spacecraft, fully procedural, and mod-friendly.

💡 Key Takeaway:
It’s modular + rule-based + styled that makes procedural ships actually readable as spaceships. You don’t need hundreds of handcrafted ships; a small library of hulls + modules + procedural rules can generate infinite unique ships that look polished.

struct ShipInterior {
    uint32_t id;
    std::vector<uint32_t> moduleSockets;  // IDs for modules / rigs
    std::vector<std::string> zones;       // Crew zones, engineering, weapons bay
    std::string role;                      // Default role if fully equipped
};

struct ShipHull {
    uint32_t id;
    PCGIdentity identity;                  // procedural seed
    std::string hullClass;                 // Frigate, Cruiser, etc.
    ShipInterior interior;
    std::vector<ModuleDef> modules;        // procedural modules installed
    std::vector<RigDef> rigs;
    std::string role;                       // Assigned once fully outfitted
};

Procedural Placement Rules:

Only compatible modules can occupy a socket

Role specialization biases module generation

Example:

if (ship.interior.HasFreeSocket("Weapons")) {
    ModuleDef weapon = GenerateWeaponModule(ship.PCGSeed, career, faction);
    ship.modules.push_back(weapon);

🚀 Core Principle (NovaForge Ship Generation)
The ship is not generated from the outside in.
The ship is generated from the inside out.

Interior layout → determines:

What modules can exist

Where mass, power, and function live

How the ship must look externally

What role the ship becomes when complete

1️⃣ Ship Generation Order (Critical)
This order is non-negotiable if you want ships to feel real:

PCG Seed
  ↓
Interior Layout
  ↓
Functional Zones
  ↓
Module & Rig Population
  ↓
Ship Profile (mass, power, heat, role)
  ↓
External Geometry Assembly
  ↓
Final Role Lock + Bonuses
Most games do this backwards. NovaForge does it correctly.

2️⃣ Interior Layout = The Ship’s DNA
An Interior Layout is not cosmetic.
It is a functional blueprint.

Example interior layout definition:

struct InteriorLayout {
    InteriorClass type; // Industrial, Combat, Exploration, Logistics
    int weaponBays;
    int engineBays;
    int cargoBays;
    int utilityBays;
    int crewCapacity;
    float internalVolume;
};
This single struct already determines:

What the ship can physically support

What roles are even possible

How big / wide / long the hull must be

A ship with:

3 cargo bays + refinery bay
cannot look sleek
It must be bulky — and the generator enforces that.

3️⃣ Functional Zones → External Silhouette
Each interior zone projects outward into geometry requirements.

Mapping rule (this is the key):
Interior Zone	External Requirement
Engine Bay	Rear-mounted exhausts, thrust symmetry
Cargo Bay	Bulges, container racks, flat surfaces
Weapon Bay	Hardpoints, turrets, barrel exposure
Utility Bay	Antennas, sensor dishes, protrusions
Crew Zones	Windows, lighting clusters
So the generator does not ask:

“What should this ship look like?”

It asks:

“What must exist to support what’s inside?”

4️⃣ Ship Profile Emerges (Not Assigned)
After interiors + modules are placed, the ship gains a profile:

struct ShipProfile {
    float mass;
    float powerDraw;
    float heatOutput;
    float cargoVolume;
    float combatRating;
    float industrialRating;
    float explorationRating;
};
This profile is computed, not chosen.

This is crucial:

Two ships with the same hull class can end up wildly different

Profile drives handling, acceleration, warp cost, visibility

Profile affects NPC perception and AI behavior

5️⃣ External Geometry Is a Consequence
Only after the interior and profile exist do we assemble the exterior.

External assembly rules:
Hull length scales with internal volume

Width scales with cargo + crew density

Engine count scales with mass & thrust requirements

Weapon hardpoints appear only where weapon bays exist

Utility protrusions appear only if utility modules exist

This prevents:
❌ “Tiny ship with massive cargo”
❌ “Sleek scout ship with refinery inside”
❌ Visual lies

6️⃣ Role Lock-In (The Payoff)
A ship does not start with a role.

A role is earned when requirements are met:

bool IsMiningSpecialist(const Ship& ship) {
    return ship.profile.industrialRating > 0.75f &&
           ship.HasModule("OreProcessor") &&
           ship.HasModule("MiningLaser");
}
When a role locks:

Passive bonuses activate

NPCs recognize the role

UI labels the ship accordingly

Visual accents can intensify (lights, decals, color bias)

Examples:

Dedicated Miner → yield bonus, reinforced hull visuals

Fleet Combat Vessel → shield regen, aggressive silhouette

Deep Explorer → scan range, sensor-heavy exterior

Logistics Carrier → trade bonuses, massive cargo profile

This feels earned, not selected from a menu.

7️⃣ Why This Feels “Like a Real Ship”
Because real ships (and vehicles) work this way:

Submarines

Cargo freighters

Warships

Space stations

All are:

Designed around interior function first

NovaForge ships:

Look believable

Behave consistently

Are readable at a glance

Are impossible to min-max into nonsense

You do not need:

Finished ship meshes

Fancy interiors

FPS walkable spaces (yet)

You do need:

3️⃣ Interior Generation (SpaceWorld Style)
Your SpaceWorld PCG already does this pattern:

Seed → Zones → Populate → Resolve Rules
We reuse it verbatim for ships.

enum class InteriorZoneType {
    Engine,
    Cargo,
    Weapons,
    Utility,
    Crew,
    Industrial,
    Sensors
};
Interior PCG flow:

InteriorWorld GenerateShipInterior(uint64 ShipSeed) {
    InteriorWorld world;
    world.zones = GenerateZones(ShipSeed);
    world.modules = PopulateModules(world.zones, ShipSeed);
    return world;
}
This is exactly how your SpaceWorld generates belts, anomalies, stations — just scaled down.

4️⃣ Interior → Ship Profile (Simulation Layer)
Once the interior exists, SpaceWorld resolves simulation properties.

This is where the ship becomes “real”:

ShipProfile ResolveShipProfile(const InteriorWorld& interior) {
    ShipProfile p;
    p.mass = Sum(zone.volume * density);
    p.power = Sum(module.powerDraw);
    p.heat = Sum(module.heat);
    p.cargo = Sum(cargoZones.capacity);
    return p;
}
This mirrors:

Planet mass calculation

Station throughput calculation

Resource density resolution

Same PCG pattern, different scale.

5️⃣ Interior → Exterior (Form Follows Function)
Here’s the part you specifically asked for.

Exterior is NOT generated freely
It is derived from the resolved interior.

Rules:

Interior Outcome	Exterior Consequence
Large engine zones	Multiple or oversized thrusters
High cargo volume	Wide hull, protruding containers
Many weapon zones	Visible hardpoints
Sensor-heavy	Antennas, spines, dishes
Crew-heavy	Windows, light clusters
In SpaceWorld terms:

Exterior geometry is just a visualization of simulation truth

Same way:

Planet radius visualizes mass

Atmosphere color visualizes composition

6️⃣ Role Emergence (Not Assignment)
Roles work like planet types or station archetypes.

They are classified after generation, not chosen.

ShipRole ClassifyRole(const ShipProfile& p) {
    if (p.cargo > X && p.industrial > Y)
        return ShipRole::IndustrialMiner;

    if (p.combat > X && p.shields > Y)
        return ShipRole::CombatVessel;

    if (p.sensors > X && p.warpEfficiency > Y)
        return ShipRole::Explorer;

    return ShipRole::Generalist;
}
Once classified:

Bonuses apply

NPC AI treats the ship differently

UI labels it

Factions value it differently

This mirrors:

System security classification

Planet habitability classification

    case EntityType::Anomaly:

        GenerateShipVisual(e);
        break;

Important:
This fork happens after PCG, not during PCG.

Ship seeds stay identical

SpaceWorld logic stays untouched

Only visuals diverge

This preserves determinism.

2️⃣ “No-art” ship assembler using primitives (this will instantly fix visuals)

struct HullFrame {
    float length;

};

Capsule or stretched box

Aligned to +Z (forward)

int engineCount = Clamp(profile.mass / MASS_PER_ENGINE, 1, 6);

for (int i = 0; i < engineCount; i++) {
    AttachCylinder(
        position = RearOfHull + RadialOffset(i),

    );

1️⃣ EXACT PSEUDOCODE
Replace your current ship mesh call (surgical, safe, deterministic)
❌ What you have now (conceptual)
void BuildEntityVisual(Entity& e) {
    Mesh mesh = GenerateNoiseMesh(e.seed, e.mass, e.radius);
    e.visual.mesh = mesh;
}
✅ Replace with this (NO behavior change for non-ships)
void BuildEntityVisual(Entity& e) {
    switch (e.type) {
        case EntityType::Asteroid:
        case EntityType::Anomaly:
        case EntityType::Debris:
            e.visual.mesh = GenerateNoiseMesh(e.seed, e.mass, e.radius);
            break;

        case EntityType::Ship:
            e.visual.mesh = BuildShipMesh(e);
            break;
    }
}
That’s the entire fork.
PCG seeds stay intact. World gen stays intact.

BuildShipMesh() — authoritative ship visual path
Mesh BuildShipMesh(Entity& ship) {
    ShipProfile profile = ship.profile;   // derived from interior
    ShipVisualAssembler assembler(profile);

    assembler.AddHull();
    assembler.AddEngines();
    assembler.AddCargo();
    assembler.AddWeapons();
    assembler.AddSensors();

    return assembler.Build();
}
No noise. No marching cubes. No displacement. Ever.

2️⃣ DEBUG SHIP BUILDER (UNDER 1 HOUR, GUARANTEED)
This is intentionally primitive-only and stupid simple.

Core data structures
struct ShipProfile {
    float length;
    float hullRadius;

    int engineCount;
    float cargoVolume;
    int weaponSlots;
    int sensorSlots;

    ShipRole role;
};
ShipVisualAssembler (single file, no engine magic)
class ShipVisualAssembler {
public:
    ShipVisualAssembler(const ShipProfile& p)
        : profile(p) {}

    void AddHull();
    void AddEngines();
    void AddCargo();
    void AddWeapons();
    void AddSensors();

    Mesh Build();

private:
    ShipProfile profile;
    MeshBuilder builder;
};
Hull (10 minutes)
void ShipVisualAssembler::AddHull() {
    builder.AddCapsule(
        center = Vec3(0, 0, 0),
        radius = profile.hullRadius,
        length = profile.length,
        axis = Axis::Forward
    );
}
Engines (15 minutes)
void ShipVisualAssembler::AddEngines() {
    float spacing = profile.hullRadius * 0.6f;

    for (int i = 0; i < profile.engineCount; i++) {
        Vec3 offset = RadialOffset(i, profile.engineCount, spacing);

        builder.AddCylinder(
            position = RearOfHull(profile.length) + offset,
            radius = profile.hullRadius * 0.25f,
            length = profile.hullRadius * 0.6f,
            direction = -Forward
        );
    }
}
Cargo bulges (10 minutes)
void ShipVisualAssembler::AddCargo() {
    if (profile.cargoVolume <= 0)
        return;

    int pods = Clamp(profile.cargoVolume / 50, 1, 6);

    for (int i = 0; i < pods; i++) {
        builder.AddBox(
            position = BottomSideOffset(i),
            scale = Vec3(1.2f, 0.6f, 0.6f) * CargoScale(profile.cargoVolume)
        );
    }
}
Weapons (10 minutes)
void ShipVisualAssembler::AddWeapons() {
    for (int i = 0; i < profile.weaponSlots; i++) {
        builder.AddCylinder(
            position = TopHardpoint(i),
            radius = 0.1f,
            length = 0.4f,
            direction = Forward
        );
    }
}
Sensors (5 minutes)
void ShipVisualAssembler::AddSensors() {
    for (int i = 0; i < profile.sensorSlots; i++) {
        builder.AddSphere(
            position = NoseOffset(i),
            radius = 0.15f
        );
    }
}
Build (5 minutes)
Mesh ShipVisualAssembler::Build() {
    builder.ApplyMaterial(RoleMaterial(profile.role));
    builder.ComputeNormals();
    return builder.Finalize();
}
⏱ Total time: ~55 minutes, including compile errors.

3️⃣ WHERE INTERIORS LIVE (THIS IS CRITICAL)
This is where most engines screw up. You won’t.

The Golden Rule
Interiors are authoritative.
Visuals are a projection.

Memory layout (clean & future-proof)
Entity
├── Transform
├── ShipInterior   ← authoritative
├── ShipProfile    ← derived (cached)
├── Visual         ← generated from profile
└── Systems (AI, physics, etc.)
ShipInterior (never touches rendering)
struct ShipInterior {
    int engineBays;
    int cargoBays;
    int weaponBays;
    int crewQuarters;
    int sensorRooms;

    float internalVolume;
};
Derivation step (pure function)
ShipProfile DeriveProfile(const ShipInterior& interior) {
    ShipProfile p;

    p.engineCount  = interior.engineBays;
    p.cargoVolume  = interior.cargoBays * 50.0f;
    p.weaponSlots  = interior.weaponBays;
    p.sensorSlots  = interior.sensorRooms;

    p.length       = BaseLength + interior.internalVolume * 0.05f;
    p.hullRadius   = sqrt(interior.internalVolume) * 0.15f;

    p.role         = InferRole(interior);

    return p;
}
🚨 This function is deterministic, cacheable, and testable.

Update flow (when interior changes)
void OnInteriorChanged(Entity& ship) {
    ship.profile = DeriveProfile(ship.interior);
    ship.visual.mesh = BuildShipMesh(ship);
}
That’s it. No cross-contamination.

Long answer: you don’t want the generator to “learn” in a neural-net sense. You want it to record design intent as structured data and then generalize from that data deterministically.

What you’re describing is basically example-driven procedural generation (sometimes called authoring by demonstration). Let’s lock that in cleanly so it doesn’t turn into spaghetti later.

🧠 Mental Model (important)
Think of your debug ship builder as a Ship Pattern Authoring Tool, not a one-off editor.

You are not placing meshes.
You are authoring constraints, relationships, and proportions.

The generator never copies ships.
It samples from the space defined by your examples.

1️⃣ What the generator actually “learns”
Not pixels. Not meshes.

It learns rules extracted from your placements.

Example (what you place)
Engine pods are:

always behind center of mass

evenly distributed radially

never intersect cargo

scale with engine count

That becomes data, not code.

Core concept: Ship Pattern
struct ShipPattern {
    string name;

    HullPattern hull;

    vector<ModulePattern> engines;
    vector<ModulePattern> cargo;
    vector<ModulePattern> weapons;
    vector<ModulePattern> sensors;

    PatternConstraints constraints;
};
This is what your debug mode records.

2️⃣ Debug Builder = Pattern Recorder
In debug mode, every object you move is annotated, not freeform.

struct ModulePattern {
    ModuleType type;

    // relative to hull, not world
    Vec3 localPosition;
    Vec3 localDirection;

    // normalized scale (important!)
    Vec3 scaleRatio;

    // semantic tags
    vector<Tag> tags;
};
Key idea:
📌 Everything is stored in normalized hull space
(not absolute meters)

When you move something in debug mode
void OnDebugMove(Module& m) {
    ModulePattern& p = currentPattern.Find(m.id);

    p.localPosition = NormalizeToHull(m.position);
    p.localDirection = m.forward;
    p.scaleRatio = m.scale / hull.baseScale;
}
You’re not editing a ship.

You’re editing a rule template.

3️⃣ Extracting rules automatically (this is the magic)
When you save a pattern, you derive constraints from it.

PatternConstraints AnalyzePattern(const ShipPattern& p) {
    PatternConstraints c;

    c.engineRadialSymmetry =
        DetectRadialDistribution(p.engines);

    c.engineRearOnly =
        All(p.engines, IsBehindCOM);

    c.weaponTopsideBias =
        AverageNormal(p.weapons).dot(Up) > 0.6f;

    c.cargoAvoidsEngines =
        MinDistance(p.cargo, p.engines) > Threshold;

    return c;
}
This is rule inference, not AI.

✔ deterministic
✔ debuggable
✔ modifiable

4️⃣ How generation uses your examples
When generating a new ship:

ShipPattern base =
    PatternLibrary.Sample(role, sizeClass);

ShipProfile profile =
    DeriveProfile(interior);

ShipVisualAssembler assembler(profile);

assembler.ApplyHull(base.hull);

assembler.PlaceModules(
    base.engines,
    profile.engineCount,
    base.constraints
);

assembler.PlaceModules(
    base.cargo,
    profile.cargoVolume,
    base.constraints
);

return assembler.Build();
So instead of:

“Generate random engines”

You get:

“Generate engines like this, but scaled, mirrored, shuffled, and validated”

5️⃣ Variation without chaos (critical)
Variation comes from parameterized mutation, not randomness.

ModulePattern Mutate(
    const ModulePattern& src,
    RNG& rng
) {
    ModulePattern out = src;

    out.localPosition +=
        rng.Range(-0.1f, 0.1f) * HullRadius;

    out.scaleRatio *=
        rng.Range(0.9f, 1.1f);

    out.localDirection =
        RotateWithinCone(src.localDirection, 5deg);

    return out;
}
Constraints are re-checked after mutation.

If violated → resample.

This is how you avoid blob ships.

6️⃣ Multiple examples = design language
The more ships you build in debug mode, the stronger the generator gets.

PatternLibrary
├── Industrial_Freighter_A
├── Industrial_Freighter_B
├── Light_Combat_Scout
├── Heavy_Combat_Brawler
├── Exploration_Frame
Generation becomes:

Pattern p = Blend(
    Sample(A),
    Sample(B),
    weight = 0.3f
);
This is how you get coherent families of ships.

{

B) Module semantic placement (this is the gold)
From any ship source (games, models, diagrams), extract:

    "avoid": ["cargo", "habitat"],
    "minDistance": 0.15
  }

This is what your system actually “learns”.

C) Intent tags (hugely important)
Tags tell the generator why something is placed.

"tags": [
  "rear-mounted",
  "high-thrust",
  "maintenance-access",
  "thermal-exhaust"
]
Without tags, learning collapses into noise.

{

IntendedRole: Industrial
SizeClass: Medium
Notes: "Long hull, rear engines, central cargo spine"

  AspectRatio: 3.4        # length / width
  Symmetry: Bilateral     # None / Bilateral / Radial
  ProfileBias: Elongated  # Stubby / Balanced / Elongated
  StructuralSpine: Yes
C. Interior Zoning (this is critical)
InteriorZones:
  - Name: Engineering
    RelativePosition: Rear
    VolumeRatio: 0.20

  - Name: Cargo
    RelativePosition: Center
    VolumeRatio: 0.45

  - Name: Habitat
    RelativePosition: Front
    VolumeRatio: 0.15
These zones will drive exterior shape later.

Modules:
  - Type: Engine
    Count: 3
    Placement:
      Region: Rear
      Distribution: Triangular
      VerticalBias: Center
    Constraints:
      Avoid: [Cargo, Habitat]
      MustFace: Back
    Tags:
      - HighThrust
      - ExhaustCritical

  - Type: CargoBay
    VolumeRatio: 0.45
    Placement:
      Region: Center
    Tags:
      - StructuralCore
E. Exterior Expression Rules
(How internals affect the outside)

ExteriorRules:
  - If: Cargo.VolumeRatio > 0.4
    Then:
      HullBulge: Center
      SurfaceDetail: ReinforcedPlating

  - If: Engine.Count >= 3
    Then:
      ExhaustPorts: Multiple
      RearProfile: Wide
This is why ships stop looking like blobs.

  - Industrial

  - NonAerodynamic
These tags are pattern selectors later.

G. Valid Variation Range
Variation:
  LengthScale: [0.9, 1.3]
  EngineCount: [2, 4]
  CargoRatio: [0.35, 0.55]
This defines how far the generator is allowed to drift.

H. Sanity Constraints (anti-blob rules)
Constraints:
  - EnginesBehindCOM
  - CargoNotExposed
  - NoModuleIntersection
  - MaintainSymmetry
✅ Outcome
This worksheet becomes one pattern input.

🧩 2️⃣ Pattern JSON Importer (Engine-Side)
This converts worksheets → engine-usable data.

A. Canonical Pattern JSON Schema
{
  "patternName": "Industrial_Freighter_Medium_A",
  "role": "industrial",
  "sizeClass": "medium",

  "hull": {
    "aspectRatio": 3.4,
    "symmetry": "bilateral",
    "profileBias": "elongated",
    "structuralSpine": true
  },

  "interiorZones": [
    {
      "name": "Engineering",
      "region": "rear",
      "volumeRatio": 0.20
    },
    {
      "name": "Cargo",
      "region": "center",
      "volumeRatio": 0.45
    }
  ],

  "modules": [
    {
      "type": "engine",
      "count": 3,
      "placement": {
        "region": "rear",
        "distribution": "triangular"
      },
      "constraints": ["avoid:Cargo", "face:back"],
      "tags": ["HighThrust", "ExhaustCritical"]
    }
  ],

  "exteriorRules": [
    {
      "if": "Cargo.VolumeRatio > 0.4",
      "then": {
        "hullBulge": "center",
        "surfaceDetail": "reinforced"
      }
    }
  ],

  "variation": {
    "engineCount": [2, 4],
    "cargoRatio": [0.35, 0.55]
  },

  "constraints": [
    "EnginesBehindCOM",
    "NoModuleIntersection"
  ],

  "designIntent": [
    "Industrial",
    "Durable"
  ]
}
B. Importer Flow (C++)
ShipPattern ImportPatternJSON(const Json& j) {
    ShipPattern p;

    p.name = j["patternName"];
    p.role = ParseRole(j["role"]);
    p.sizeClass = ParseSize(j["sizeClass"]);

    p.hull = ParseHull(j["hull"]);
    p.interiorZones = ParseZones(j["interiorZones"]);
    p.modules = ParseModules(j["modules"]);

    p.exteriorRules = ParseExteriorRules(j["exteriorRules"]);
    p.variation = ParseVariation(j["variation"]);
    p.constraints = ParseConstraints(j["constraints"]);

    p.intentTags = ParseTags(j["designIntent"]);

    ValidatePattern(p);
    return p;
}
C. Validation (this saves your sanity)
void ValidatePattern(const ShipPattern& p) {
    Assert(Sum(p.interiorZones.volumeRatio) <= 1.0f);
    Assert(p.hull.aspectRatio > 1.0f);
    Assert(!p.modules.empty());
}
Invalid patterns never load.

/ShipGen
 ├── Patterns/
 │    ├── industrial/
 │    │    ├── freighter_medium_A.json
 │    │    └── freighter_medium_B.json
 │    ├── combat/
 │    └── exploration/
 ├── PatternImporter.cpp
 ├── PatternValidator.cpp
 ├── PatternLibrary.cpp
Mods drop files into /Patterns.

No recompiles.

2️⃣ Interior → Hull Deformation Math
(This is why ships stop looking like blobs)

🔹 Concept: Zones as Density Fields
Each interior zone contributes a scalar field over hull space.

float ZoneInfluence(Vec3 p, Zone z) {
    return z.volumeRatio *
           SmoothFalloff(distance(p, z.center));
}
🧮 Hull Radius Function
Instead of a static hull:

float HullRadius(Vec3 direction) {
    float base = BaseHull(direction);

    float deformation = 0;
    for (Zone z : zones)
        deformation += ZoneInfluence(direction, z);

    return base * (1.0 + deformation);
}
Examples
Large cargo zone → central bulge

Engineering zone → reinforced rear

Habitat → smooth front taper

This is continuous, not boolean.

🔧 Engine / Weapon Imprints
Modules punch local modifiers:

if (module.type == Engine)
  hull -= ExhaustCutout(direction);

if (module.type == Weapon)
  hull += MountHardpoint(direction);
That’s how silhouettes emerge.

3️⃣ Pattern Visualizer Overlay
(Your most important debugging tool)

You need to see intent, not geometry.

🔍 Toggleable Overlays
A) Zone Heatmap
Cargo = orange

Shows volume dominance.

B) Constraint Lines
Red lines = violations

Green = valid distances

Yellow = borderline

Example:

Engine ❌ too close to Cargo
C) Influence Vectors
Arrows showing:

Mass bias

Thrust axis

COM

This tells you why the hull looks the way it does.

D) Variation Ghosts
Show 5 generated variants at once:

[Base] [Var A] [Var B] [Var C] [Var D]
If any look wrong → fix the pattern, not the generator.

4️⃣ Damage + Mutation Rules
(This is where NovaForge becomes special)

Damage does NOT destroy meshes.
It deforms patterns.

💥 Damage Model
DamageEvent {
  location: Vec3
  magnitude: float
  type: Thermal / Kinetic / EMP
}
🧬 Pattern Mutation
if (DamageHits(zone:Engineering)) {
    pattern.engineEfficiency *= 0.85;
    pattern.exteriorRules += "ExposedPlating";
}
Hull deformation updates live.

🔁 Salvage / Refit Loop
Player rebuilds ship →
Pattern mutates →
Future ships of that class inherit bias.

PatternStats["industrial"].reinforcedBias += 0.1;
This is evolution, not randomness.

🧠 Why this whole system works
Debug UI authors rules

What you’re asking for is a “inside-out ship generator”:

You sketch interior intent → the engine wraps a hull around it, meshes it, and textures it automatically.

Below is the exact architecture that makes this work cleanly, with no artists and no hand-authored hulls.

🧠 Core Idea (lock this in)
Interiors are authoritative.
The hull is a derived artifact.

You never edit:

hull vertices

exterior meshes

silhouette manually

You only define:

interior spaces

connections

functional modules

Everything else is math.

What you do in the Debug Editor (very simple)
You place interior volumes, not walls.

InteriorBlock {
    Type: Cargo | Engine | Habitat | Corridor | WeaponMount
    Size: Vec3        // relative, not meters
    Priority: float   // structural importance
    Tags: []
}
That’s it.

You can literally think in LEGO blocks.

1️⃣ Debug Editor UX (what it feels like)
🧱 Interior Layout View (primary)
Orthographic slice views (Top / Side / Front)

Grid-snapped blocks

Color-coded by type

[ HAB ]—[ COR ]—[ CARGO ]—[ ENG ]
No hull visible yet.

Type dropdown

Size sliders

Importance (Structural ↔ Utility)

Optional tags:

“Pressurized”

“Exposed”

“Reinforced”

🚫 Things you never do
No vertex editing

No mesh sculpting

No texture painting

No manual symmetry

2️⃣ Turning interior layout → hull shape
This is the key algorithm.

Step A: Convert interior to a density field
Each interior block contributes a scalar field.

float DensityAt(Vec3 p) {
    float d = 0;
    for (Block b : blocks)
        d += b.priority * SmoothBoxFalloff(p, b.bounds);
    return d;
}
Think of it like:

Cargo = heavy

Engines = heavy + directional

Corridors = light

Step B: Generate hull envelope (the “shrink-wrap”)
The hull surface is an isosurface around density.

HullSurface = IsoSurface(
    DensityField,
    threshold = HullThickness
);
This guarantees:

Everything fits

No wasted empty hull

Shape follows interior mass

This alone fixes your “blob” problem.

Step C: Apply functional bias
Now you bias the hull based on function:

if (Block.Type == Engine)
    PullHullRearward();

if (Block.Type == Cargo)
    ExpandLaterally();

if (Block.Type == Habitat)
    SmoothCurvature();
This is why:

freighters get bellies

fighters get narrow spines

explorers get smooth noses

3️⃣ Meshing the hull (automatic, stable)
You generate hull mesh from the isosurface.

Important:
You mesh in normalized hull space, not world space.

Pipeline:

Voxel field (coarse)

Marching Cubes / Dual Contouring

Edge relaxation

Symmetry enforcement

LOD extraction

You only need one mesher for all ships.

4️⃣ Exterior detail = projection, not placement
You never “place” exterior greebles.

You project them from interior meaning.

Examples
Engine block → exhaust
ProjectExhaust(
    from = EngineBlock.center,
    direction = -Forward,
    radius = EngineBlock.size.x
);
Cargo block → paneling
AddSurfaceDetail(
    region = CargoInfluenceArea,
    type = ReinforcedPlating
);
Weapon mount → hardpoint
StampHardpoint(
    normal = WeaponFacing,
    size = WeaponClass
);
Exterior details are imprints, not objects.

5️⃣ Texturing (no artists, still good)
Textures are procedural layers, not UV art.

Each hull point samples:

Material = Blend(
    BaseHullMaterial,
    ZoneMaterialInfluence,
    DamageOverlay,
    WearNoise
);
So:

Engineering → darker, scorched

Cargo → plated, worn

Habitat → smooth, clean

One material system. Infinite ships.

6️⃣ Pattern JSON this produces (clean & small)
Your editor exports only interior data:

{
  "interiorBlocks": [
    { "type": "Habitat", "size": [1,1,1], "priority": 0.6 },
    { "type": "Cargo", "size": [3,2,2], "priority": 1.0 },
    { "type": "Engine", "size": [2,2,1], "priority": 1.2 }
  ],
  "constraints": ["Symmetric", "EnginesRear"],
  "variation": {
    "scale": [0.9, 1.2]
  }
}
Hull never appears in JSON.
That’s intentional.

7️⃣ Why this produces “real” ships
Because real ships are designed:

around machinery

around mass

around function

You’re accidentally doing actual naval architecture, just procedurally.

All for free.

You said:
i want to make a modular spaceship generation system for a game i am making  C++ open GL

ChatGPT said:
Oh hell yes — this is a great problem to tackle, and it fits perfectly with the kind of games you’ve been talking about (Avorion / EVE-ish / sandbox sim vibes).

I’ll walk you through a clean, scalable modular spaceship generation system that works well in C++ + OpenGL, and won’t paint you into a corner later when you add AI, damage, or networking.

Big Picture (mental model first)
Think of a ship as three layers:

Gameplay Ship
 ├── Logical Structure (modules, stats, power, damage)
 ├── Spatial Structure (attachment graph / grid)
 └── Render Representation (OpenGL meshes)
Generation happens top-down:

Decide what kind of ship this is

Assemble modules into a valid structure

Derive stats + visuals from the same data

1️⃣ Core Concept: Ship = Graph of Modules
Each ship is not a single mesh — it’s a graph of modules.

Module Definition (C++)
enum class ModuleType {
    Core,
    Engine,
    Weapon,
    Hull,
    Cargo,
    Shield,
    Utility
};

struct Hardpoint {
    glm::vec3 localPosition;
    glm::vec3 direction;
    bool occupied = false;
};

struct ModuleDef {
    ModuleType type;
    std::string meshId;
    float mass;
    float powerDraw;
    float powerOutput;

    std::vector<Hardpoint> hardpoints;
};
🔑 Hardpoints are the secret sauce — they let modules snap together without grids or voxels.

2️⃣ Ship Instance (Runtime Structure)
struct ModuleInstance {
    ModuleDef* def;
    glm::mat4 transform;
    int parent = -1;
    std::vector<int> children;
};

struct Ship {
    std::vector<ModuleInstance> modules;
};
This lets you:

Detach modules

Destroy sections

Traverse for power, damage, AI targeting

3️⃣ Generation Pipeline (THIS is the important part)
Step 1: Pick a Ship Archetype
struct ShipArchetype {
    int minWeapons;
    int maxWeapons;
    int minEngines;
    float targetMass;
    float aggressiveness;
};
Examples:

Interceptor → light, engines first

Freighter → cargo + hull spam

Battleship → core + weapons ring

Step 2: Start with a Core Module
Every ship begins with one core.

int coreIndex = addModule(coreDef, glm::mat4(1.0f));
No core = no ship.

Step 3: Grow the Ship (Procedural Assembly)
This is basically controlled BFS:

std::queue<int> openModules;
openModules.push(coreIndex);

while (!openModules.empty() && ship.modules.size() < maxModules) {
    int current = openModules.front();
    openModules.pop();

    for (auto& hp : ship.modules[current].def->hardpoints) {
        if (hp.occupied) continue;

        ModuleDef* next = pickModuleForHardpoint(hp, archetype);
        if (!next) continue;

        int child = attachModule(current, hp, next);
        openModules.push(child);
    }
}
This gives you:

Organic shapes

Structural logic

No floating nonsense

4️⃣ Avoiding “Ship Spaghetti”
Hard Rules (non-negotiable)
✔ Engines must face backward
✔ Weapons must have firing arcs
✔ Power must balance
✔ Mass must not exceed thrust

if (totalPowerDraw > totalPowerOutput)
    rejectShip();

if (thrust < mass * MIN_ACCEL)
    rejectShip();
Regenerate until valid.

5️⃣ Rendering in OpenGL (Clean + Fast)
Each module has:

One mesh

One local transform

GPU Instancing (recommended)
struct InstanceData {
    glm::mat4 model;
};

std::vector<InstanceData> instanceBuffer;
Render by mesh ID:

for (auto& batch : meshBatches) {
    glDrawElementsInstanced(...);
}
This scales to hundreds of ships easily.

6️⃣ Damage, Destruction, and Fun Stuff 😈
Because your ship is a graph:

Destroy module → detach subtree

No power → weapons go offline

Core destroyed → ship dead

void destroyModule(int index) {
    for (int child : modules[index].children)
        destroyModule(child);

    modules[index].def = nullptr;
}
This is why modular beats monolithic meshes every time.

7️⃣ Optional: Grid / Block Variant (Avorion-like)
If you want Minecraft vibes:

Use a 3D grid

Each block = module

Merge blocks into meshes at runtime

Same logic systems still apply

8️⃣ Folder Structure (Future-Proof)
/Ship
  Ship.h
  ShipGenerator.cpp
  ModuleDef.h
  ModuleDatabase.cpp
  ShipStatsSystem.cpp
  ShipRenderSystem.cpp
Why This Scales REALLY Well
✔ Procedural generation
✔ Player building
✔ AI ships using same system
✔ Networking friendly
✔ Damage & salvage “just work”

You’re basically laying the foundation for:

without the tech debt.

“I want No Man’s Sky–style ship silhouettes and classes, but hard-coded, modular, and readable, not random soup.”

That’s exactly how Hello Games does it under the hood — it looks procedural, but it’s actually rule-driven kitbashing with strict silhouette logic.

Let’s lock this down cleanly and in a way that’s friendly to C++.

🧠 High-Level Idea (NMS-style, demystified)
No Man’s Sky does NOT free-form generate ships.
It does this instead:

Ship Class
 ├── Fixed core module
 ├── Mandatory silhouette modules
 ├── Optional decorative modules
 ├── Hard limits on symmetry, length, width
You’re going to do the same — but better 😄

1️⃣ Ship Classes = Silhouette Blueprints
Each ship class defines what MUST exist and where.

Example ShipClass Definition
enum class ShipClass {
    Fighter,
    Explorer,
    Hauler,
    Shuttle,
    Exotic
};

struct SilhouetteRule {
    glm::vec3 minExtent;
    glm::vec3 maxExtent;
    bool symmetricX;
};

struct ShipClassDef {
    ShipClass type;
    SilhouetteRule silhouette;

    int minWings;
    int minEngines;
    int minWeapons;

    float aggressiveness;
    float cargoBias;
};
This alone prevents ugly ships.

2️⃣ Module Taxonomy (CRITICAL)
Modules are not just “Engine” or “Weapon”.

They are style + role + silhouette contributors.

enum class ModuleRole {
    Core,
    Spine,
    Wing,
    Engine,
    Weapon,
    Cargo,
    Cockpit,
    Fin,
    Ornament
};

enum class StyleTag {
    Industrial,
    Sleek,
    Alien,
    Chunky,
    Retro
};
Module Definition (extended)
struct ModuleDef {
    ModuleRole role;
    std::vector<StyleTag> styleTags;

    glm::vec3 bounds;
    std::vector<Hardpoint> hardpoints;

    float mass;
    float thrust;
    float cargo;
    float firepower;
};
🔑 Style tags are how the AI keeps ships coherent.

3️⃣ The AI Ship Designer (Brain Flow)
The AI doesn’t “randomly add parts”.
It follows a design script.

Design Phases (in order)
1. Pick ShipClass
2. Pick Style Theme
3. Place Core + Spine
4. Add Silhouette Modules
5. Add Functional Modules
6. Add Ornamentation
7. Validate & Adjust
4️⃣ Step-by-Step Heuristics (Concrete)
Step 1: Pick Class + Style
ShipClassDef* cls = pickShipClass(factionBias);
StyleTag style = pickStyleForFaction(faction);
NMS does style locking early — don’t mix too much.

Step 2: Place Core + Spine (Non-negotiable)
attach(core, spineForward);
attach(core, spineRear);
This gives:

Ship length

Directionality

Weapon + engine anchors

Step 3: Enforce Symmetry FIRST
For fighters & explorers:

if (cls->silhouette.symmetricX) {
    attachMirrored(Wing, leftHardpoint);
}
This is why NMS ships always feel “designed”.

Step 4: Silhouette Modules Before Function
Example rules:

Class	Must Have
Fighter	Wings, visible engines
Hauler	Cargo pods, wide body
Explorer	Long spine, minimal wings
Shuttle	Compact, stubby
ensureMinModules(ModuleRole::Wing, cls->minWings);
5️⃣ Function Drives Look (Not the Other Way Around)
Once silhouette exists:

while (firepower < desiredFirepower)
    addModule(ModuleRole::Weapon);

while (thrust < mass * accelTarget)
    addModule(ModuleRole::Engine);
But:

Engines snap only to rear-facing points

Weapons must have firing arcs

6️⃣ Ornamentation = Identity (Very NMS)

4️⃣ DNA Generation 🧬 (Repeatable)
ShipDNA AIShipDesigner::generateDNA(const FactionStyle& faction, uint32_t seed) {
    Random rng(seed);

    ShipDNA dna;
    dna.seed = seed;

    dna.shipClass = weightedPick<ShipClass>({
        {ShipClass::Fighter, 0.4f},
        {ShipClass::Explorer, 0.3f},
        {ShipClass::Hauler, 0.2f},
        {ShipClass::Shuttle, 0.1f}
    }, rng);

    dna.primaryStyle = randomFrom(faction.allowedStyles, rng);
    dna.symmetric = rng.nextFloat() < faction.symmetryBias;

    dna.combatRole = weightedPick(faction.combatBias, rng);

    // Base desires
    dna.desiredWeapons = rng.range(2, 6);
    dna.desiredEngines = rng.range(1, 4);
    dna.desiredCargo  = rng.range(0, 3);

    return dna;
}
5️⃣ Skeleton Build (NMS Trick #1)
void AIShipDesigner::buildSkeleton(Ship& ship, const ShipDNA& dna) {
    int core = ship.addModule(getModule(ModuleRole::Core, dna.primaryStyle));

    int spineFront = ship.attach(core, getModule(ModuleRole::Spine));
    int spineRear  = ship.attach(core, getModule(ModuleRole::Spine));

    ship.markForward(spineFront);
    ship.markRear(spineRear);
}
This locks:
✔ Length
✔ Direction
✔ Engine & weapon anchors

6️⃣ Silhouette Modules (NMS Trick #2)
void AIShipDesigner::addSilhouetteModules(Ship& ship, const ShipDNA& dna) {
    if (dna.shipClass == ShipClass::Fighter) {
        ship.attachMirrored(
            ship.core(),
            getModule(ModuleRole::Wing, dna.primaryStyle)
        );
    }

    if (dna.shipClass == ShipClass::Hauler) {
        for (int i = 0; i < dna.desiredCargo; ++i)
            ship.attach(ship.core(), getModule(ModuleRole::Cargo));
    }
}
Silhouette comes before stats.

7️⃣ Functional Assembly
void AIShipDesigner::addFunctionalModules(Ship& ship, const ShipDNA& dna) {
    while (ship.weaponCount() < dna.desiredWeapons)
        ship.attach(ship.forward(), getModule(ModuleRole::Weapon));

    while (ship.engineCount() < dna.desiredEngines)
        ship.attach(ship.rear(), getModule(ModuleRole::Engine));
}
8️⃣ Combat Role Tuning 🔥
void AIShipDesigner::tuneForCombatRole(Ship& ship, const ShipDNA& dna) {
    switch (dna.combatRole) {
    case CombatRole::Brawler:
        ship.addArmorBias();
        ship.addShortRangeWeapons();
        break;

    case CombatRole::Kiter:
        ship.addExtraEngines();
        ship.addLongRangeWeapons();
        break;

    case CombatRole::Sniper:
        ship.reduceMass();
        ship.addHighAlphaWeapons();
        break;

    case CombatRole::Carrier:
        ship.addUtilityBays();
        break;
    }
}
9️⃣ Ornamentation (NMS Trick #3)
void AIShipDesigner::addOrnaments(Ship& ship, const ShipDNA& dna) {
    Random rng(dna.seed);

    if (rng.nextFloat() < 0.3f)
        ship.attachRandom(getModule(ModuleRole::Fin));

    if (rng.nextFloat() < 0.2f)
        ship.attachRandom(getModule(ModuleRole::Ornament));
}
✨ These never affect gameplay — just identity.

🧩 1️⃣ Hardpoint Snapping Math (This Is the Core)
A hardpoint defines:

Position

Forward direction

Up vector (don’t skip this)

Mirror capability

struct Hardpoint {
    glm::vec3 position;   // local space
    glm::vec3 forward;    // attachment direction
    glm::vec3 up;         // roll reference
    bool allowMirror;
};
Each module has:

One parent hardpoint

One root hardpoint (where it attaches)

🔁 Snapping Transform (Exact Math)
Goal:

Align child.root.forward to -parent.forward
Align child.root.up to parent.up

Step-by-step
glm::mat4 computeSnapTransform(
    const Hardpoint& parent,
    const Hardpoint& child,
    bool mirror
) {
    glm::vec3 targetForward = -parent.forward;
    glm::vec3 targetUp = parent.up;

    glm::vec3 childForward = child.forward;
    glm::vec3 childUp = child.up;

    if (mirror) {
        childForward.x *= -1.0f;
        childUp.x *= -1.0f;
    }

    // Rotation: align forward vectors
    glm::quat rot1 = glm::rotation(childForward, targetForward);

    // Fix roll (align up)
    glm::vec3 rotatedUp = rot1 * childUp;
    glm::quat rot2 = glm::rotation(rotatedUp, targetUp);

    glm::quat finalRot = rot2 * rot1;

    glm::vec3 worldPos =
        parent.position -
        (finalRot * child.position);

    return glm::translate(glm::mat4(1), worldPos) *
           glm::mat4_cast(finalRot);
}
✔ No gimbal lock
✔ Deterministic
✔ Mirror-safe
✔ NMS-style snapping

🪞 Mirrored Attachments (Symmetry)
void attachMirrored(
    int parent,
    ModuleDef* module
) {
    attach(parent, module, false);
    attach(parent, module, true);
}
Mirror only at hardpoint level, never scale meshes.

🔒 Valid Attachment Rules
Reject if:

Forward vectors > 30° off

Collision bounds overlap

Firing arcs blocked

Engine exhaust obstructed

if (dot(parent.forward, -child.forward) < 0.85f)
    reject();
💥 2️⃣ Module Damage & Cascading Failures

Module Health Model
struct ModuleHealth {
    float hp;
    float maxHp;
    float armor;
    bool destroyed;
};
Power & Dependency Graph
Every module registers dependencies:

struct ModuleNode {
    int index;
    std::vector<int> dependents;
    bool powered;
};
Core → Reactors → Engines → Weapons

Damage Propagation Rules
When a module is destroyed:
void destroyModule(int idx) {
    modules[idx].health.destroyed = true;

    // Power loss
    for (int dep : powerGraph[idx].dependents)
        depower(dep);

    // Structural detachment
    detachSubtree(idx);
}
Structural Detachment (CRITICAL)
Ships are trees.

void detachSubtree(int root) {
    for (int child : modules[root].children)
        detachSubtree(child);

    spawnDebris(modules[root]);
    removeModule(root);
}
This gives you:
✔ Wings blown off
✔ Engines drifting away
✔ Salvage gameplay

Cascading Failure Examples
Hit	Result
Reactor destroyed	Weapons + shields offline
Spine destroyed	Front section detaches
Engine destroyed	Thrust imbalance
Power conduit destroyed	Local blackout

💾 Binary Ship Serializer (C++)
🎯 Goals (what we’re optimizing for)
✔ Save/load in microseconds
✔ No mesh / transform spam
✔ Deterministic regeneration
✔ Backward compatibility

We serialize intent + structure, not results.

1️⃣ What We Actually Serialize (Important)
Minimal, correct data:
ShipFile
 ├── Header
 ├── ShipDNA
 ├── ModuleOverrides (optional)
 └── DamageState (optional)
Everything else is regenerated.

2️⃣ File Header (Versioning + Safety)
#pragma pack(push, 1)
struct ShipFileHeader {
    uint32_t magic;        // 'SHIP' = 0x50494853
    uint16_t version;      // Serializer version
    uint16_t flags;        // compression, etc
    uint32_t fileSize;
};
#pragma pack(pop)
constexpr uint32_t SHIP_MAGIC = 0x50494853;
constexpr uint16_t SHIP_VERSION = 1;
✔ Versioned
✔ Extensible
✔ Detects corrupt files instantly

3️⃣ ShipDNA Serialization 🧬
This is the core identity.

#pragma pack(push, 1)
struct ShipDNABinary {
    uint32_t seed;

    uint8_t shipClass;
    uint8_t primaryStyle;
    uint8_t combatRole;

    uint8_t desiredWeapons;
    uint8_t desiredEngines;
    uint8_t desiredCargo;

    uint8_t symmetric;
};
#pragma pack(pop)
This is only 12 bytes.

🔑 This is why your system scales insanely well.

4️⃣ Module Overrides (Player / Damage / Mods)
Overrides are diffs, not full state.

enum class OverrideType : uint8_t {
    RemovedModule,
    AddedModule,
    ReplacedModule
};
#pragma pack(push, 1)
struct ModuleOverrideBinary {
    OverrideType type;

    uint16_t moduleIndex;    // index in generated ship
    uint16_t moduleDefId;    // for added/replaced

    uint8_t hardpointIndex;
    uint8_t mirror;
};
#pragma pack(pop)
Examples:

Player deletes a wing

Player adds extra engine

Damage permanently destroys module

5️⃣ Damage State (Optional, Lightweight)
Only serialize non-full-health modules.

#pragma pack(push, 1)
struct ModuleDamageBinary {
    uint16_t moduleIndex;
    uint16_t hp;   // quantized (0–65535)
};
#pragma pack(pop)
6️⃣ Full Binary Layout
[ShipFileHeader]
[ShipDNABinary]

uint16 overrideCount
[ModuleOverrideBinary x N]

uint16 damageCount
[ModuleDamageBinary x M]
No padding.
No pointers.
No STL garbage.

7️⃣ Writing the File (Save)
void saveShipBinary(
    const ShipDNA& dna,
    const std::vector<ModuleOverrideBinary>& overrides,
    const std::vector<ModuleDamageBinary>& damage,
    const std::string& path
) {
    std::ofstream file(path, std::ios::binary);

    ShipFileHeader header{};
    header.magic = SHIP_MAGIC;
    header.version = SHIP_VERSION;

    file.write((char*)&header, sizeof(header));

    ShipDNABinary dnaBin = toBinary(dna);
    file.write((char*)&dnaBin, sizeof(dnaBin));

    uint16_t overrideCount = overrides.size();
    file.write((char*)&overrideCount, sizeof(overrideCount));
    file.write((char*)overrides.data(),
               overrides.size() * sizeof(ModuleOverrideBinary));

    uint16_t damageCount = damage.size();
    file.write((char*)&damageCount, sizeof(damageCount));
    file.write((char*)damage.data(),
               damage.size() * sizeof(ModuleDamageBinary));

    header.fileSize = file.tellp();
    file.seekp(0);
    file.write((char*)&header, sizeof(header));
}
8️⃣ Loading the File (Regeneration Flow)
Ship loadShipBinary(const std::string& path) {
    std::ifstream file(path, std::ios::binary);

    ShipFileHeader header;
    file.read((char*)&header, sizeof(header));

    assert(header.magic == SHIP_MAGIC);

    ShipDNABinary dnaBin;
    file.read((char*)&dnaBin, sizeof(dnaBin));

    ShipDNA dna = fromBinary(dnaBin);

    Ship ship = AIShipDesigner().generateShip(dna);

    uint16_t overrideCount;
    file.read((char*)&overrideCount, sizeof(overrideCount));

    for (int i = 0; i < overrideCount; ++i) {
        ModuleOverrideBinary ov;
        file.read((char*)&ov, sizeof(ov));
        applyOverride(ship, ov);
    }

    uint16_t damageCount;
    file.read((char*)&damageCount, sizeof(damageCount));

    for (int i = 0; i < damageCount; ++i) {
        ModuleDamageBinary dmg;
        file.read((char*)&dmg, sizeof(dmg));
        ship.modules[dmg.moduleIndex].hp = dmg.hp;
    }

    return ship;
}

💾 1️⃣ Binary WORLD Save
(ships + stations + debris, scalable to TITANS)

Core Principle
The world save is chunked, not monolithic.
Nothing serializes “everything at once”.

🌍 World Save Structure
WorldSave
 ├── Header
 ├── SectorChunks[]
 │     ├── Ships[]
 │     ├── Stations[]
 │     ├── DebrisFields[]
 │     └── FleetRefs[]
 └── GlobalState
Each sector chunk is saved independently.

🧾 World Header
struct WorldHeader {
    uint32_t magic;          // 'WRLD'
    uint16_t version;
    uint64_t universeSeed;
    uint64_t timestamp;
};
🚢 Ship Entry (World-Level)
struct WorldShipEntry {
    uint64_t entityId;
    uint32_t dnaSeed;

    glm::vec3 position;
    glm::quat rotation;
    glm::vec3 velocity;

    uint32_t factionId;
    uint8_t sizeClass;

    uint32_t shipDataOffset; // pointer into ShipSave blob
};
➡️ The actual ship internals are stored using your Ship Binary Serializer (DNA + overrides).

🛰️ Stations = Ships With Locked Modules
Stations are ships with:

Zero velocity

Anchored cores

Non-detachable interior modules

Same system. No duplication.

☄️ Debris Fields (Ultra Cheap)
Debris is not ships.

struct DebrisEntry {
    glm::vec3 position;
    glm::vec3 velocity;
    uint16_t meshId;
    uint16_t ttl;
};
No hardpoints.
No AI.
Auto-garbage-collected.

💡 Why This Scales
✔ TITANS only serialize dirty sections
✔ Inactive sectors aren’t loaded
✔ Multiplayer replication is trivial
✔ World saves stay fast even at endgame

🧠 2️⃣ Fleet Save & AI State
A fleet is a first-class entity, not just ships near each other.

🚩 Fleet Structure
struct FleetSave {
    uint64_t fleetId;
    uint32_t factionId;

    uint64_t commanderShipId;
    std::vector<uint64_t> shipIds;

    FleetDoctrine doctrine;
    FleetOrder currentOrder;

    AIStateBlob aiState;
};
🧠 Fleet AI State (Minimal but Sufficient)
You do not save behavior trees.

You save intent.

struct AIStateBlob {
    uint8_t aggression;
    uint8_t cohesion;
    uint8_t retreatThreshold;

    glm::vec3 objectivePosition;
    uint64_t targetEntityId;
};
On load:
➡️ AI reconstructs behavior from state + doctrine.

🧠 Titan Fleet Exception

Per-sector AI states

Still chunked.

🧩 3️⃣ ModuleDef Hashing & Mod Support
(this is huge — and often done wrong)

🔑 Golden Rule
Never serialize ModuleDef IDs directly.
Serialize content hashes.

🔒 Module Definition Hash
uint64_t hashModuleDef(const ModuleDef& def) {
    Hash64 h;
    h.add(def.role);
    h.add(def.size);
    h.add(def.styleTags);
    h.add(def.bounds);
    h.add(def.hardpoints);
    h.add(def.statsBlob);
    return h.finalize();
}
📦 Serialized Module Reference
struct ModuleRef {
    uint64_t moduleHash;
    uint16_t hardpointIndex;
    uint8_t mirror;
};
🔌 Mod Loading Rules
Mods register ModuleDefs

Engine builds a hash → ModuleDef map

Missing hash?

Fallback mesh

“Unknown Tech” placeholder

Safe degradation

This prevents:
❌ Save corruption
❌ Mod conflicts
❌ Version lock-in

🧬 Backward Compatibility
Old saves load even if:

Meshes changed

Stats rebalanced

Modules visually replaced

As long as the hash resolves.

🎮 4️⃣ Undo / Redo for Ship Editor
(this is where people usually cry)

🎯 Correct Model: Command Stack
You do not snapshot ships.

You store editor commands.

🔁 Editor Command Interface
struct EditorCommand {
    virtual void apply(Ship& ship) = 0;
    virtual void undo(Ship& ship) = 0;
};
🧩 Example Commands
Attach Module
struct AttachModuleCmd : EditorCommand {
    ModuleRef module;
    int parentIndex;

    void apply(Ship& ship) override {
        ship.attach(parentIndex, module);
    }

    void undo(Ship& ship) override {
        ship.detach(module);
    }
};
Remove Module
struct RemoveModuleCmd : EditorCommand {
    int moduleIndex;
    ModuleSnapshot snapshot;

    void apply(Ship& ship) override {
        snapshot = ship.capture(moduleIndex);
        ship.remove(moduleIndex);
    }

    void undo(Ship& ship) override {
        ship.restore(snapshot);
    }
};
📚 Editor History
std::vector<std::unique_ptr<EditorCommand>> undoStack;
std::vector<std::unique_ptr<EditorCommand>> redoStack;
✔ Infinite undo
✔ Deterministic
✔ Network-safe (commands replicate easily)

🧠 Bonus: Symmetry Undo
Symmetry just issues multiple commands grouped as one transaction.

beginTransaction();
attachLeft();
attachRight();
endTransaction();

System	Benefit

Fleet Save	Strategic continuity
Module Hashing	Mod safety + longevity
Undo/Redo	Player trust & creativity
All four:
✔ Deterministic
✔ Scalable
✔ Titan-safe
✔ Multiplayer-ready

🚩 1️⃣ Fleet Doctrine Editor (Player + AI)
Core Idea

AI factions

Doctrine Data Model
struct FleetDoctrine {
    // Formation & movement
    float preferredRange;        // km
    float cohesion;              // 0..1
    float flankBias;             // -1..1
    float pursuitAggression;     // 0..1

    // Engagement rules
    float retreatThreshold;      // hull %
    float targetFocus;           // 0..1 (spread vs focus)
    bool protectCapitals;
    bool screenCarriers;

    // Target priorities
    std::vector<TargetPriority> priorities;
};
struct TargetPriority {
    TargetClass target;
    float weight;
};
Doctrine Examples
“Line Battle”
Tight formation

Focus fire

Protect capitals

Low pursuit

“Wolfpack”
Loose cohesion

High flank bias

Kill stragglers

Fast retreat

“Titan Escort”
Absolute cohesion

Screen missiles

Sacrifice frigates first

Doctrine templates

1️⃣ How Module OBJ Files Are Structured (Rules First)
Before generating anything, every module OBJ must follow rules or procedural assembly breaks.

Coordinate & Orientation Rules (NON-NEGOTIABLE)
Rule	Value
Forward	+Z
Up	+Y
Right	+X
Origin	Center of mass
Units	1.0 = 1 meter
Symmetry plane	X = 0
Hardpoints in OBJ (Simple & Robust)
Hardpoints are named empties, not geometry.

Naming convention

hp_<role>_<index>
Example:

hp_engine_0

✅ Can raw OBJ files be used directly for ship generation?
Yes, if all of these are true (yours already are):

✔ Known forward/up axes

AI / player → assemble graph
Renderer → draw merged transforms

🧠 How Ship Generation Actually Works (Runtime)
Think of ship assembly as transform math, not mesh editing.

1️⃣ Load Module Mesh Once
struct ModuleMesh {
    Mesh gpuMesh;
    std::vector<Hardpoint> hardpoints;
};
You load each OBJ one time, cache it, and reuse it forever.

2️⃣ Parse Hardpoints from OBJ
When loading the OBJ:

Any object named hp_* is treated as a hardpoint

Its vertex position = local snap point

Orientation inferred from naming or default forward (+Z)

struct Hardpoint {
    std::string name;
    glm::vec3 localPos;
    glm::quat localRot;
};
⚠️ You do not render these objects.

3️⃣ Snap Modules Together (Core Logic)
When attaching Module B to Module A:

worldTransformB =
    worldTransformA
  * hardpointA.transform
  * inverse(hardpointB.transform);
That’s it.
No mesh modification. No welding. No CSG.

4️⃣ Final Ship = Transform Graph
struct ShipNode {
    ModuleMesh* mesh;
    glm::mat4 localTransform;
    std::vector<ShipNode*> children;
};
Rendering just walks the graph and draws meshes with transforms.

This is exactly how LEGO-style ship games work.

🎮 AI-Generated Ships vs Player Ships
Important Insight
AI ships and player ships use the same pipeline.

Only the decision-making differs.

System	Difference
AI ship design	Chooses modules automatically
Player editor	Player chooses modules
Renderer	Identical
Physics	Identical
Damage	Identical
This is a huge win.

💥 What About Physics & Collisions?
You have 3 sane options (pick one early):

Option A — Per-Module Collision (Recommended)
Each module has its own simple collider

Damage is localized naturally

Works great for capitals & Titans

Option B — Per-Ship Aggregate
Combine bounding boxes at runtime

Cheaper, less accurate

Option C — Hybrid
Capitals use per-module

Small ships use aggregate

Raw OBJs don’t block any of these.

🧠 1️⃣ Exact C++ Hardpoint Snapping Math (with Mirroring)
Coordinate Assumptions (lock these)
+Z = forward

+Y = up

+X = right

Hardpoints are local to their module

Hardpoint Data
struct Hardpoint {
    std::string name;
    glm::vec3 localPos;
    glm::quat localRot; // optional, identity if not authored
    bool mirrored;      // true if _L/_R
};
Core Snap Formula (This Is the Money Shot)
To attach child module B onto parent module A:

glm::mat4 snapTransform(
    const glm::mat4& parentWorld,
    const Hardpoint& parentHP,
    const Hardpoint& childHP)
{
    glm::mat4 parentHPWorld =
        parentWorld *
        glm::translate(glm::mat4(1.0f), parentHP.localPos) *
        glm::mat4_cast(parentHP.localRot);

    glm::mat4 childHPInverse =
        glm::inverse(
            glm::translate(glm::mat4(1.0f), childHP.localPos) *
            glm::mat4_cast(childHP.localRot)
        );

    return parentHPWorld * childHPInverse;
}
✔ No mesh modification
✔ Deterministic
✔ Works for all scales

🔁 Mirroring Logic (Left / Right)
When snapping *_L to *_R, flip X axis in child space:

glm::mat4 mirrorX()
{
    return glm::scale(glm::mat4(1.0f), glm::vec3(-1, 1, 1));
}
Apply before childHP inverse:

glm::mat4 childHPInverse =
    glm::inverse(
        mirrorX() *
        glm::translate(glm::mat4(1.0f), childHP.localPos) *
        glm::mat4_cast(childHP.localRot)
    );
That’s it. No special cases.

🛠️ 2️⃣ Minimal OBJ Loader (Modules Only)
This loader ignores everything you don’t need.

What We Parse
v → vertices

f → faces

o hp_* → hardpoints

Data Structures
struct ObjMesh {
    std::vector<glm::vec3> vertices;
    std::vector<uint32_t> indices;
    std::vector<Hardpoint> hardpoints;
};
OBJ Parse Skeleton
ObjMesh loadModuleOBJ(const std::string& path)
{
    ObjMesh mesh;
    std::string line;
    std::ifstream file(path);

    Hardpoint* currentHP = nullptr;

    while (std::getline(file, line)) {
        std::stringstream ss(line);
        std::string token;
        ss >> token;

        if (token == "o") {
            std::string name;
            ss >> name;
            if (name.starts_with("hp_")) {
                mesh.hardpoints.push_back({name});
                currentHP = &mesh.hardpoints.back();
            } else {
                currentHP = nullptr;
            }
        }
        else if (token == "v") {
            float x, y, z;
            ss >> x >> y >> z;

            if (currentHP)
                currentHP->localPos = {x, y, z};
            else
                mesh.vertices.push_back({x, y, z});
        }
        else if (token == "f") {
            uint32_t a, b, c;
            ss >> a >> b >> c;
            mesh.indices.insert(mesh.indices.end(), {a-1, b-1, c-1});
        }
    }
    return mesh;
}
⚠️ You do not render hardpoints.
⚠️ This is enough for procedural ships.

💥 3️⃣ Per-Module Damage & Cascading Failures
This is where your ships feel real.

Module Health Model
struct ModuleState {
    float hp;
    float maxHp;
    bool powered;
    std::vector<int> connectedModules;
};
Damage Flow
void applyDamage(int moduleId, float dmg)
{
    auto& m = modules[moduleId];
    m.hp -= dmg;

    if (m.hp <= 0 && m.powered) {
        m.powered = false;
        cascadeFailure(moduleId);
    }
}
Cascading Failures (Simple & Effective)
void cascadeFailure(int moduleId)
{
    for (int neighbor : modules[moduleId].connectedModules) {
        if (modules[neighbor].powered) {
            modules[neighbor].powered = false;
        }
    }
}
Results:

Lose engine → thrust drops

Lose reactor → weapons offline

Lose spine → sections detach

No scripting needed.

🎮 4️⃣ Ship Editor UX (Graph-Based)
The editor is not a mesh editor — it’s a graph editor.

Core Concepts
Nodes = modules

Edges = hardpoint connections

Transform preview = snapping math above

Editor Flow
Select hardpoint on ship

Show valid compatible modules

Ghost-preview snap

Confirm → add node to graph

Undo = remove node

Undo / Redo (Command Pattern)
struct EditorCommand {
    virtual void apply() = 0;
    virtual void undo() = 0;
};
struct AttachModuleCmd : EditorCommand {
    int moduleId;
    void apply() override { attach(moduleId); }
    void undo() override { detach(moduleId); }
};
Scaling UX for Capitals
Zoom snaps by size tier

Collapse modules into “sections”

Titans show zones, not parts

🌍 5️⃣ Titan Chunk Streaming Rules
Titans are not one ship — they’re world chunks.

Titan Structure
Titan
 ├── Section A (city)
 ├── Section B (engine wall)
 ├── Section C (weapon spine)
 └── Section D (dock)
Each section:

Owns its own module graph

Simulates independently

Streams separately

Streaming Distance Rules
Distance	Loaded
> 200 km	proxy only
50–200 km	low-fidelity sim
< 50 km	full modules
Interior	FPS-level detail
Section Activation
void updateTitanStreaming(const glm::vec3& playerPos)
{
    for (auto& section : titan.sections) {
        section.active =
            distance(playerPos, section.center) < section.loadRadius;
    }
}
Save Implication
Each section:

Saves independently

Can be destroyed independently

Persists damage forever

🧠 1️⃣ Fleet AI Using Module Health (Not Just HP)
Core Idea
Fleets react to capability loss, not raw damage.

A ship at 80% hull but missing engines behaves very differently from one at 40% hull with full systems.

Ship Capability Snapshot
Each ship periodically derives a capability profile from its modules:

struct ShipCapability {
    float mobility;    // engines online / total
    float firepower;   // weapons online / total
    float power;       // reactors online / total
    float command;     // bridge / sensors status
};
Updated every few seconds, not every frame.

Fleet AI Decision Hooks
Fleet AI queries capability deltas, not absolute values:

if (ship.capability.mobility < 0.4f)
    assignEscort(ship);

if (ship.capability.firepower < 0.3f)
    pullFromLine(ship);

if (ship.capability.command == 0.0f)
    downgradeToAutonomous(ship);
Emergent Behavior (Free Gameplay)
Crippled ships fall back automatically

Fleets protect damaged capitals

Killing sensors blinds fleets

Destroying reactors causes panic retreats

No scripting. Just math + thresholds.

🧱 2️⃣ Interior FPS Module System (Ship-as-Level)
This is where your game becomes special.

Rule #1 (Critical)
Interior modules = same modules, different representation

No duplicate data.

Module → Interior Mapping
struct InteriorZone {
    ModuleID sourceModule;
    ZoneType type;      // Habitat, EngineRoom, Hangar
    BoundingVolume volume;
};
Each exterior module:

Optionally exposes an interior

Has a prefab interior layout

Shares health & power state

Damage ↔ Interior Loop
Exterior Event	Interior Result
Engine hit	Fire, decompression
Reactor damaged	Radiation zone
Hull breach	Zero-G + vacuum
Power loss	Lights out, doors fail
FPS gameplay directly influences ship combat:

Sabotage reactors

Repair engines

Capture bridges

Titans are interior worlds:

Multiple gravity zones
